Oh, sons, because I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by the fulfillment of it, its end is achieved in it, and by its expositions and declarations, it is my view that you complete it with this treatise, which is a part of the operation with me, so it is surgery.
Because the operation with me is spread in our region and in our time completely private, while many risk the science of it, and its vestiges are held hostage; and there remain of it only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which muddle the most and lead to those errors and uncertainties because their clauses are understood and the force of it and the art are lengthened.
And it is seen to me that you bring to life that with the order of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and for that it may come with the forms of the tools of cautery and the other instruments of the work as it is for the addition of the declaration, and for the preparation of it, which cause is necessary.
But the cause for which no good artificer with his hand is found in this our time, is that the art of medicine is long, and that it compels its actor to be exercised before that in the science of anatomy, which reconciles Gualia because it knows the movements of the members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and to have the knowledge of the bones, nerves and arteries, the number of them, and the egress of them; and of the pulsatile and quiet veins, and the places of the emission of them.
And for that reason, Hippocrates says that in a name is little, but in the work much, and most of all in the art of the hand.
But now we have already said that in the introduction of this book; because whoever is not aware of what we have said of anatomy, is not evacuated that he does not fall into error for which many men die, so as I have seen many of those, some are formed in this science and cast themselves away from that science and their experiments.
Because I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician who had incised a scrophulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and incised some arteries of the neck so that much blood flowed while the woman died among the hands of him.
And I saw another physician who prescribed that the patient swallow a stone from a man who had processed in his state, and the stone was large, and it came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that the man on the third day died.
And I had already been called to extract that, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient, I knew above him that he would die.
And I saw another physician who was taking a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and he was given a black crutch who was broken in his thigh; and prepared the physician with his ignorance, I extracted the fracture, on the wound, with splints and splints with strong structure, and did not leave the wound to breathe.
And then he set it according to his desires, and he reattached it, and then he left it for some days and commanded that we untie the ligament; and it was so that the thigh and the foot became festered, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I appeased him to untie the ligament, and achieved tranquility and then eased his pains.
But however, the corruption had already been confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and it did not cease to wander the corruption in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated after some days, until the malice of it was magnified to its lord.
The cause of which is that the cancerous caut is pure of melancholic humor, so that it does not come to it with iron of all, but that it be in a limb the one which suffers that of all it be held hostage.
And therefore, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with me be devised in two divisions: so it is in the operation which is associated with health, and in the work with which there is peril in the major dispositions.
And I have already established this in every place of this book in which I present an operation in which there is error and fear; because it is necessary for you to guard this and leave that, so that the foolish do not find a way to speak and to dishonor you.
And therefore, take your arms with diligence and with the protection of God, and your patients with facility and with firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God, pursuing health and praiseworthy success, and leave the terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and hold your arms from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not escape you: because more remains to your glory, and more in the world and in the end to your sanctities.
Gualia already says in some of his monitions: "Do not treat a bad illness, so that you are not called bad physicians."
And I have already divided this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and the first of the first part. The first chapter, ordered by the head, about the forms of instruments and the tools of cauteries, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of suction and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of poultices, and of the extraction of arrows and of things similar to those, all chaptered and ordered by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the cure of sprains and things similar to those; chapter ordered by the head, and of the forms of the instruments.
Know that you should say the quality of the judgments of that cautery and its harms, and in what completion cauterization should be prohibited.
Therefore I say that the sermon of the quality of the judgment of cauterization and its harm is a long and prolix sermon, and subtle by science, and secretly hidden; and already many wise men have spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and to me a little of their sermons suffices, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cauterization agrees with every universal composition, whether it is of matter or of its matter, except for two compositions, which are compositions of matter that is cauda.
In the composition that is dry and of matter, they disagree; because some say that cauterization is beneficial to that, and others say the contrary, that cauterization is not convenient in illness that is by quality and by dryness, because the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that illness that is dry and by dryness, with that which is caut and dry, be cured.
And I say to those who say the contrary, that by cauterization is done benefit in all dry and  illnesses that come in the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of a man and its humors to the composition of fire, you find the body of man to be cold.
And I say for the sermon of this, that experience has already revealed to me on some occasions.
But however, it should not come upon this that which is already exploited and exercised in the chapter of cauterization by the previous exercise, and who has known the diversity of the composition of men and the disposition of the illnesses in their own bodies, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the duration of those.
But in the other compositions, there is no fear over you, and most of all in the cold and humid illnesses, because all the physicians have agreed upon those, and they are not disagreed about the judgment with cauterization in those.
And know, sons, that of the secret of cure by cauterization with fire and of its goodness, it is because fire is simple, the action of which does not reach beyond the member that is cauterized and not to the other member attached to it, except for a small harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches that in the members that are far from it, and forcefully makes them bring illness of difficult healing and forcefully a great pain.
But fire, by its sublimity and of its substance goodness, does not do so except that it is superfluous. And already it is known to us by experience and by the length of service of study in the art about the truth of the causes; and for that reason, I am excused for the length of the sermon, and if it were not that it is not written in my book in this pause to you, the secret hidden with fire, and the quality of its operation in the bodies, and its expulsion by which it expels the illnesses, with a philosophical and demonstrative sermon, which would be acute in your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time in which it is convenient to do cauterization and they pause the best time of spring.
And I say that cauterization is convenient at all times, because so that the harm from the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is cauterized, and most of all if the cauterization is for pains or of necessary pains, strong and fistulous, which do not suffer delay because it is feared that those that come malice, which is greater than a small harm entering by time.
And therefore do not catch in your minds, or, sons, that this is not esteemed by the vulgar people and the foolish physicians, so it is that to that illness that is cured by cauterization is not reduced at all times, and they pause that consequence.
And the cause is not at all so as they care about that, because cauterization is nothing more than in place of medicine that alters the composition and dries up the humors which are the cause for which pains come.
But however, cauterization is superfluous over medicine by the will of its validity and virtue of its operation and greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that its illness may return at any time, according to the composition of the patient and the location of their illness, and the virtue of it, and what is prepared in the body of it of the aggregation of superfluities in it, and what has been received to it in its acquisition in foods and things similar to those causes; unless that illness in which cauterization is administered is a subtle illness in a member of few superfluities and humors, as cauterization of teeth for pain and things similar to that, it is possible that in it that pain does not return, but that it is made in small amounts.
And more commonly it is said that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is rightly said, for those who understand it, because they believe that it is not a cure that benefits with medicine nor with any other cause except cautery.
And the cause is the opposite of that; and the understanding that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when they administer the ways of healing in some illnesses, and those medicines do not confer, and then at the end of the cause they administer cautery, and it benefits to that. And therefore, it is said that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which the common people and many foolish doctors understand.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not make a scar, as it is not absolutely true, because I have already seen that and I have found that this does not do so except in some bodies, and cautery with this is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity that you want by the roar of the gold, and that it prepares to receive the burning; and if you adjust over it, the gold melts by the fire, and in the fire some plates so that the artisan catches for the occupation of that.
And for this reason, cautery with iron must be done more lightly and more carefully to the rectitude of operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head within the feet, so that later the demander asks for what he wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers to the humidity and coldness over the nerves affected by the cause of which there are two, so it is the cause of soda and multitude of rheumatic flux, called corissa, from the head to the parts of the ears and the auricles flow, and multitude of sleep, and pain of teeth and of the throat, and universally of all illness that comes by coldness, as well as paralytically, soda and apoplexy, and similar in those illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this, that first you command the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine the head modifying, for three or for four nights, according to that you require the virtue of the illness and the age of him and his custom.
And then command him to shave his head, and then do that seizure between your hands on a low bench and that he place his hands on his feet.
And then place the razor of your palm near the nose between his eyebrows. But however, as the tie of the middle place has been reached, sign that place with a tenta, and then ignite the cautery olive of which this is the form:
And then place that upon the spot marked with the tenta, in the manner that descends, with which your hand be constricted little by little, and you revolve that.
And then lift your hand slightly and look at the place, and if you see already of the bone being discovered some quantity of the head, as well as the quantity of a grain of errs, then elevate your hand, and if not turn your hand with that iron, with another of that, if it is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve that on the place and leave that for three days.
And then place on it a cloth soaked in butter and leave that on it until the scar of the fire is destroyed. And then cure the place with anguent tetrafarmacon until it is healed.
And already they said that when the plague remains open, so much better it gets the scar, for that it is better and more useful.
And they said some of them that it be cauterized the spine within the bone, and it be held the cautery until it is burned any part of the appearance of the bone.
And then be scraped that which is burned of the bone, and then be cured.
And they said others that with the cautery arrive to the back, until it presses in the bone strongly until the bone breaks, and this is the form of alphecati small: And they estimate of that place expire the vapors of the head.
And it be kept the wound open for long time, and then be cured until it is consolidated.
But to me, these two kinds of cautery have not been seen at all, except in some men according to way of fear; and leaving that, to me, it is better for health, when it is; because the head is devitalized by solution of continuity of it as we have testified in the other members, and more so when the head of the patient is of a cold nature.
And the species of cautery first is more safe and better to me; and of that I use and with that work with health.
Second chapter.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head and it is prolonged in the patient, and they use herbs, and potions, and purgatives, and plasters, and guats, and cadels, and gualinas, and semblants, and mostly if it has already been cauterized with a cautery that they recount and does not benefit them in any of those causes, then consider that if the head of the patient is of strong creation in its nature; and it is not frail and finds a great deal of heat, then cauterize it with another cautery besides that one a little. Then cauterize that one on the horn of their head with a cautery until the bone is parted. Incise the bone and uncover the quantity which we have recounted, and cauterize that one with a cautery in the back part of the head in the location which is called vas, — and that is the location in which a cupping glass is placed. And alleviate your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, because the patient through this finds a great deal of pain different from the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head.
And you say this cautery in its location, because whoever cauterizes with the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is more subtle cautery than that with which the middle location of the head is cauterized; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
Of cauterization of old-fashioned migraine.
When pain is felt in the side of the head, or pain is extended into the eye, it should be evacuated with medications that modify the head, and another cure should be administered, which I have said in the divisions of the illnesses; and if nothing benefits, then cauterization in it is in two ways: either cauterization with acute and burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with burning medicine is that you take a piece of alum, and you cut and separate the extremities of it from both ends. And then you incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet until there is a location made there below the bone, until it is hidden.
And then you strengthen over it with a stopper by descending constriction, and leave it for the space of three hours; and then you untie it and take out the alum, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then you place over it a poultice submerged in butter until the location makes a scab.
And then cure this with an enema until it is healed.
And if you want to, do this with some burning medicines which I have written in the two treatises of burning medicines.
But the cauterization with iron according to this manner: ignite a cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a clavilh cautery, because the head of it is as the form of a clove in which there is some curvature, and in the middle location of it is a small eminence.
And then place it over the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the artery which is below is not burned — and revolve the iron little by little with speed.
And if the quantity of it which is burned by the proximity of the bone is so much that the majority of it, then lift your hand so that the artery which is below is not burned, so that there does not come a flow of blood.
And then imbibe a poultice in salt water and place that over the location, and leave it for three days; and then place a poultice mashed with butter, and then cure with an enema until it is healed.
And if you want to, cauterize with the extremity of the knife-like cautery, and guard yourself from incision of the artery in this migraine properly old-fashioned.
Chapter Four.
Of cauterization of old migraine.
When you cure migraine with what we have said of the previous cure, and what we have said in the divisions of the illnesses, and if it does not benefit the cure, and sometimes because of the illness that does not suffice to it what we have said of the first cauterization with medicine or of the cauterization with fire, then you must ignite the knife-like cautery until it is white-hot, because you signify over the location of the pain with a line of length of which the majority of it is said, or that which it is; and hold your hand for one time and strengthen it until you incise the artery and it reaches into the bone.
However, it is necessary to you that you guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is bad for chewing, and that the lacert is not burned and the nerve which feels that, and that it does not become a spasm.
And if it is about the custody and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, because in the incision of it there is fear, and mostly to that one who ignores what it does and is not studious and experienced; and leaves the operation in it at the beginning.
And I will come with remembrance of the regiment of the flow of blood accident of the artery according to its manner, in its location of this book.
And if you see of this illness that this cauterization does not suffice and you see the body of the patient tolerable, cauterize that in the middle location of the head as we have recounted, and cure the wound until it is healed.
And if you are going to administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the cauterization of the artery, use a cautery with two handles, and this is a better cauterization and more convenient.
Chapter V.
Of cauterization of ear pains.
When in the ear there is pain due to heat and it is cured with laxative causes and with the other cure which I have mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is the one which has a point, of which this is the form:
And when you point it to the ignition, and it is moved around the entire ear like a wheel is rotated, or in the circuit of whichever side if the pain is on whichever side. Lengthen the cautery from the root of the ear little by little as much as you feel the location with your finger.
And if the cautery of ten points in the whole ear, and that one, and then cure the places while they are healing.
Chapter VI.
Of cauterization of tumors.
The tumor which is cured and by cauterization is only of the kinds which come from phlegm, according to what we have said in the divisions of the diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the kind that comes from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you cure that kind of tumor with poultices and purges and washings, and if you do not find your cure convenient, then you must cauterize the tumor with three cauteries: the first at the root of the ear, the second a little below its temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And pause your cautery for the opposite of the diseased part, because softening does not come except in the part that is healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize that with the cautery in the right of the extremity of the ear, as sovereign a little above the corner of the head, and in the temple another, the length of which should be according to the length of the finger, and pause your hand with the cautery until the quantity of the majority of the species of the cord is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a kind of cutlery, the form of which is shown opposite. But however more subtle is that one a little as you see; and where the handle in it of superfluous thickness a little.
And then cure the place with that which is shown opposite until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
Of cauterization of old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old and you cure that with the water which we have said and you do not find your medication convenient and the ailment does not have fever, then cauterize that with four cauteries: over all the horns of its head; and with the cautery and the middle place of the head, as we have said; and with the cautery in the cheekbone. And the manner of the cautery is made according to what has been said before.
And it is already cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and it is more behind; and then it is cured with the water which proceeds.
Chapter VIII.
Of cauterization of oblivion made by phlegm.
When it is necessary to give the patient at the beginning large poultices and pills of the kind that are modified.
And then shave the head of the patient and place on the cheekbone of him a plaster of the kind described in the treatise of plasters.
And place that several times, for that is the manner of cauterization. And do that according to that same order which I have already said.
And therefore if by those he is healed, well; and if not, then make to him three cauteries in the rear part of his head, which are descending from the most sovereign part of the head into the part more below the neck. And pause between each cautery the thickness of the finger, and then cure the cautery with the water which proceeds.

Then cure that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive according to the form given before.
Chapter IX.

Chapter X.
Of cauterization of epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized, unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, if you modify the brain of him at the beginning with years and with the other cure which we have said in the division when the patient is great and tolerates the excess of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate the medicines, then administer guarguarissmes and mastiguatoris to the brain, before that for a few days, with the blessing of the foods.
And then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize him with a cautery in the middle location of the head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery in the occiput of him, and on each horn of his head cauterize. And this olive cautery according to what has been preceded.
But if the patient is a child, then pause a subtle cautery according to this manner.

Chapter XII.

Chapter .xvi.

Chapter XVII.
Of cauterization of fistula that is in the lacrimal of the heel.
When you cure a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of the diseases and do not confer your cure, then you must cauterize it according to this manner: command the patient to place his head on your knee, and hold the head of him some minister between your hands in such a manner that it is not disturbed nor agitated with his head.
And then place a cloth infused in oil or in a poultice of ptilli on the heel of him.

Chapter XX.
Of cauterization of the teeth and of softening of the blood vessels.

Chapter XXI.
Of cauterization of tooth pain.
When tooth pain is due to cold, or a worm is in it, or it does not respond to treatment with medicine, then cauterization is done in that tooth in two ways: cauterization with a cow's horn, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with horn is that you take a cow's horn and heat it in a blacksmith's forge, and then take a handle and wrap it around the tip of the horn, and then dip that in the heated horn, and place it on the painful tooth firmly while it is heated.
And then repeat this several times until the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or a stick in the heated horn below, and place it on the painful tooth, and place a heated iron over it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
But however, cauterization with fire is that you attend to the channel of iron or of fire, and it be in the heart of it some thickness and the sharpness of the fire does not reach the mouth of the patient.
And then ignite the cauterizer, the shape of which will come, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand over it while the cauterizer is heated; and do this several times because the pain will be relieved either that day or the next.
And because of the impression of that cauterizer, the patient gargles with good broth and keeps it in their mouth for an hour and then spits it out; and this is the shape of the cauterizer.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish according to what is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:

Chapter XXII.
Of the cure of alconati.
This illness which is called alconati is nothing but a swelling which is adjusted in the wound resembling descending water and it is not that.
But the manner of working in it is that you make the patient lie on the saddle arranged and straight, and then take its head in your hands of two parts and hold it so that you see the swelling coming to the parts below, and then let it dry and not descend, and stay the light, and the patient sees the causes as he sees.
And if it does not descend below, you know that it is water.
But if it is not prepared the descent of the swelling with the water that we have said, then make the patient lie between your hands and then take a subtle spatula and finish it on that over the panniculus, carefully the continuation of the conjunctiva with the cornea tunic in the crown (in the place void), until the swelling is expelled.
Therefore, as it is expelled, then you distill into the wound water cauda in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have already boiled fennel and honey.
And then cure it with the other causes until it is healed.
Chapter XXIII.
Of the cure of the descending water from the wound.
We have already said the kinds of water in the division and which ones it is convenient to pierce with a sermo, declaring, then according to the health.
Now, it is necessary that you make the patient lie between your hands on a crate and tie his wound shut, tightening it.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his wound if the wound in which is the water is the left one; or with your right hand, if the wound is the right one.
And then with your right hand take the almond, if the wound is left; or with your left hand, if the wound is right.
And then place the end of the almond near the crown for the thickness of the ray in the album of the wound from the part of the lower lacrimal, and then press the almond with virtue and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the album of the wound and feel that the almond has already arrived at the void cause.
And it is necessary that the measure of the path of the almond to the edge is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, and it is the crown of the wound; because you will see with that pupil the vision of the wound through the clarity of the cornea tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where is the water, and then you throw it down once, then another, and therefore if the water descends quickly, then wet the wound with water over which he has his sight quickly, and almond in his wound.
And then rest for a little; therefore if the water has descended, then you remove that other time before the almond is thrown.
And therefore when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and roll it with your hand little by little, and then dissolve in the water a little clear salt of gemma and with that wash the wound carefully; and then place outside the wound cotton or stopper or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white and lick the wound with water.
But now in those who are of our time place on that commi trissat with egg white.
And if you do not obey your almond to the entrance of the wound because of its hardness — because it is some of the men whose wound is very hard — then it is necessary that you take the spatula which is called alberid of which this is the form:
And therefore pierce with that that juncture, and do not end your hand in the track, and it is not if you do not make a way for the almond to a subtle place.
And then introduce the almond according to what we have said.
And you must complete the work, have the patient prepared to sleep comfortably in a dark room, where he sleeps on his back, and be prohibited from all movement.
And keep him in nature so that his nature is cleansed, and do not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And bind him according to his disposition on the third day.
And then unbind him in that dark room and expose him to the fresh air, and make him see some causes, and then return the bandage on the seventh day.
And do not allow him to do this during the hours of healing or as soon as the infusion. You must however abstain from this because the water can leak slightly through a great and vehement regard.
But if an abscess occurs, then you must unbind the wound before the seventh day, and rectify it with water that will cause the abscess to drain.
Then you unbind his sight and place a cloth over his face so that his movement is regulated, leaving him in the dark room for several days.
And then gradually remove him from that room and converse with him about his causes.
And know that in alchemy the disciple is not excused from vision some times, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And we have now arrived at some of those who are in the Artach, and some say that they make fire in the Ayrac magdaam perforated with which the water is sucked.
But however, I have not seen any in our land who has perfected water, nor is water mentioned in some of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is novel.
And these are the forms of the species of magdaan so that it may be on those who ignore those:
And they are made of copper or iron properly, and their extremities are with this triangular subtlety, the acute extremity thus as it is the form.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of flesh wounds in the nose.
When in the nose various fleshy growths appear, of which some are caused by a thing resembling a scorpion of small weight, or a polyp; and this ailment is called a polyp because it resembles that weight represented. And of this flesh is cancerous, pale, of a dark color; and of this flesh is the lion no of a dark color.
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy growths lion not fraudulent nor cancerous, you must have the patient sit between your hands and put in the sunlight, and dry his nostrils, and pick your own on those flesh. And then extract that to the outside parts, and then incise that which you have comprehended of those with a sharp, subtle spatula from one side, so that you know that all the flesh has already been removed; if any cause of which the excision is possible remains of it, then you root that with one of the subtle instruments with ease so that nothing of it remains. But if the bleeding should worsen or an abscess occurs, then you pause to him what you must; or if it is of the malignant abscesses, then you cut and cauterize that so that it may be excised, and all the cavities are removed.
And then be careful to put in the nose after the incision a mixture of wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and from it proceeds humidity into the palate, then you know that it is already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you know that deep within it is flesh wound in the pus that soars bones of the nose to which the excision has not reached.
Therefore, then you must introduce a needle of lead into which there is some thickness, and sew in it small nuts, and place between each nut the quantity of the finger or less, and engage the patient and introduce one extremity of the needle into the nose with the ray or with another cause which is possible to it.
And as soon as that is done, that it may have its odor reach into the channels of the nose, and be spit onto his throat, for many times the children in schools do this action, and it is easier cause to him who wants to do it.
And then place the two extremities of the needle, one extremity that sticks out over the nose and the other that remains in the nose, and then administer a lock of flesh with the nuts that are in the needle, and do this so that you know that the flesh is already with the nuts of the needle.
And then extract the needle, and place in the nose after the abstraction of blood a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is remaining of the flesh.
And at the end of the cause, place in the nose a lead tube for several days until it is healed.
But if you find any cure that requires it, use that.
And this is the form of the instrument a capipurge maker with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nose:
And make it of silver or copper, which is similar to a small open lamp, as a small conch, and the meat similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the instrument to be used for purging should be flat, uncovered; to which handle at the back of him, with which you hold that when you warm in him oil or water that you want of the suckable causes.
Chapter XXV.
Of the ills of the nose in the extremities.
Many times the ailment of the nose in the extremities is magnified and mentioned on certain days when it is not even worth looking at.
And for that reason, you should investigate that at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. And then place on the spot a cautery or medicine that is burning, which was in the place of it.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, dry, of a dark color and of little feeling, then do not touch that with iron for it is a foul abscess; for many times to him who incisions this abscess, great malice comes upon him who had it.
And if the abscess is of a light touch and not of a dark color, and sometimes when incisions is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and incision it without fear, and cure the spot with water that is warm and compresses until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Of the suturing of the nose and of the lips and of the ear when the continuity is dissolved by plague or by likenesses.
Know that when accidents of dissolution of continuity occur in one of these cartilages, few confer in that operation unless in some men.
Therefore, when it is necessary to do so, consider if it is fresh plague with its blood, and then you adjust the two lips of the wound with suture, and then care for it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each one is made healthy, then you should excise each part of its exposed edge until blood comes out. And then adjust the two lips with suture and strengthen them, and over the powder, place a piece of diaphano plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave this tied for two or three days, and then untie it and change the plaster and leave it until the threads are by themselves abscinditz, and then care with a rinse until it is healed.
But the manner of the suture is that you add the solution of continuity or with needles as we have recommended in the suturing of the belly, or add the solution of continuity with thread as you have been taught.
Chapter XXVII.
Of the extraction of those causes that come in the two lips in the parts of the mouth.
Many times there must be in the mouths of men in the parts of their lips some abscesses hard like fat of worms, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you should convert the lips and make incisions each one of those hardnesses from all sides, and then the abscess, fill the place with powdered sage until the blood is restrained, and then wash it with vinegar and cure the place with water that is astringent, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Of incision of the flesh adjusted in the gums.
Greatly often there is on the gum flesh adjusted, which the ancients called an albulus, therefore, you should suspend that with tweezers or what you have with the forceps and incision that, you should remove its root, and let the blood run.
And then place on the spot powdered sage or other astringent or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incision that secondarily, and cauterize that, for the cautery will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the reason of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth of the parts of the mouth and of the front and between the gums are adjusted rough, jagged, and some times black scabs, and are made yellowish or green as the gums are affected by this corruption and the teeth become jagged.
And therefore, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place the head of him in your lap, and scrape the teeth where those scabs and causes appear like sand until none of them remain, and this do of the blackness and the yellowishness and of the others until they are modified.
Therefore, if that is what is in the beginning of the root, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the root over those the next day and the second and the third until it comes to the last statement in what you want.
And know that the teeth have need of various forms and figures of mortars according to how it is prepared for your operation. And because the razors which the tooth is razored from the root are different from the razors which are razored between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of razors you shall prepare as you shall see forthwith:
Chapter .xxx.
Of the eradication of teeth.
Know that you cure the tooth of its pain with all diligence and take your time in the eradication of it, for there is no cause that should supplant the healing of it when it is eradicated, for it is substance sublimated within that the excuse in the eradication of it.
But know when the patient is forced to have that tooth eradicated, that firmly you must certify which tooth is the painful one, for often the pain deceives the patient and makes him think it is in a healthy tooth, and you eradicate that one and the pain does not cease until the bad and painful tooth is eradicated, for we have already seen this by the operation of the barbers.
Therefore, as you must certify which tooth is the painful one, and that you know well, then know that you must scarify in the circuit of the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth of the gum of all parts, and then move that with your fingers, or with subtle forceps at the beginning little by little until that you loosen it.
And then, with a strong hold, grasp it firmly with your fingers or with a strong grip, and place the head of the patient between your teeth that you well hold so that it does not hurt him, and then pull the tooth according to rectitude in a manner that you do not break it, nor give the patient greater pain.
And therefore, if it is not forgotten, then take one of those instruments, and put that one below it from all parts with facility and exert the removal of it as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or corroded, then know that you must compress with cloth that broken piece and tighten that with the extremity of a subtle ray so that it is not broken in the hour of your constriction upon it with your fingers.
And know that you must operate exquisitely with scarification in the circuit of all parts of the gum and guard much your strength that you do not break that and remain a part of it, and return upon the patient with malice that is greater than the first pain of it.
And guard yourself that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their fisting and audacity over the eradication of theirs, because they do not administer what we have recommended; for many times they make come upon the men maladies great ones which are more difficult to be extracted, and the roots of them remaugulated all, or some of those, or what you extract that with some bones of the mandible as many times as we have testified.
And after the eradication of it, the mouth be washed with wine caut or wine agre with salt.
And if it should flow of blood thereby, as many times it should, then take some quantity of zegi and sprinkle it upon the wound, and if you do not confer that zegi, cauterize.
The form of the forceps and subtle fingers with which the tooth is pulled at the beginning should be of long extremity, of short handle and thick, so that they are not bent when you must operate with them.
And be so as sometimes of thick handle so that when you tighten with them they are not bent, be short extremities.
And be made of Indian iron, descentment made of straight extremities, and in the extremities of those be teeth of which some introne in the others and tighten by strong constriction descent, and some times the extremities of those be the form of the beak of a bird which has the name ciconia, so it is guanta, and are of strong constriction.
Chapter .xxx i.

The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of more subtle extremity.

But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is twisted, or if you can, then it is cured on that in its place with what is convenient to it of the instruments and forceps which we have said in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help gives with the gesture of which this is the shape:

Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of the prominent teeth above the others.
When the teeth are prominent not according to their natural course, for those the shape is gone, and mostly when it happens to women, and mostly to the venerable old ladies; therefore, since you consider: if a tooth is nothing near another tooth, and it is not possible to suture or liniment of it, then root that; but that it is not annexed to another tooth, then you incise that with this instrument of which this is the shape; and it is similar to a pig's snout, and it is made of Indian iron of a sharp moving extremity.
And let your incision be with which you incise that in too many days for the hardness of the tooth, and that it is not damaged another of the teeth.
And if the prominent tooth is exposed to filing, then file that with a file of Indian iron of which this is the shape:
And let it all be of Indian iron and the margin well-made; and be of small pointed points as a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth be filed little by little in many days with facility, so that no other tooth is damaged and broken. And then line that at the end, and root that with scalpels.
And if the tooth of which one part is twisted and it is hurting the tongue, then when you file that until the sharpness of that fraction is stayed and smoothed, and it is filled so that the tongue does not hurt it and the word does not corrupt.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with a filling of gold or silver.
When accidents happen to the teeth in front with concussion and removal by percussion or by fall, and the patient cannot strengthen on what it manages, and you have cured that with styptic medicines, and it has not been of any use your cure, then for so that it does not break is made in those that are strengthened with a filling of gold and not of silver, because silver becomes green and soon you can see it after a few days, but gold at no time is changed.
And let the filling be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the shape of the filling is that you take the filling and take the double of it between two healthy teeth, and then that you increase with two tortuosities of the filling between two damaged ones, or if one or many, until the filling reaches the healthy tooth of the other side. And then iterate the tamping within the part of which you began, and strengthen your hand with facility and skill so that it is not damaged.
And let your squeezing with which you strengthen the filling be of the roots of the teeth that it does not escape.
And then incise the extremities of the superfluous filling with forceps or tweezers, and add those in each place with gesture, and convert them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not cause injury to the tongue. And then smooth that as tightly as a Roman.
But if it is loosened or broken, strengthen that or those with another filling in such a manner in all its time.
And this is the shape of the teeth and the figure of the rectification of two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, as well as these tied:
And when it is done that a tooth or two are returned to their places after the fall of each one, and they are grown back as we have recounted, and that they are remaugged.
And this is done only by good artificers and wise men.
And sometimes it is fitted some cause of the bones of the cow, and it is made of them as the shape of a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is strengthened as we have said, and it remains and serves with that for a long time.
Chapter .xxxiv.
Of the incision of the ligament which comes in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament which comes from under the tongue sometimes is natural with which man is born, or accidental and caused by a plague which is already consolidated.
And the operation in it is that men open the mouth of the patient, his head existing in the tieu se; and elevates his tongue, and then incisions that ligament nerve according to the latitude within which the tongue is freed from its recess.
But if in that there is some hardness or knot, and this is due to the consolidation of plague, then you throw oil and split that by the latitude within which you have incised the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And guard yourself that no fissure is made in the skin of the flesh and that you split here the artery and blood comes.
And then wash the vestiges of the incision with water or with wine or with warm water; and then place the tongue in the mouth; so it is the way which holds the patient's tongue in each night so that it is not consolidated again.
But if blood flows, place on the location vitriol trissat; but if the blood flows, then cauterize the location with a cautery of tin, which is suitable for this. And this cure with the other causes within which it is healed.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue, resembling a small lump, which prohibits the tongue from its natural operation; and often it is magnified within which the mouth is filled.
And the operation in it is that men open the mouth of the patient, seeing in front of the sun and considers the abscess.
And if you see it of dark color or of black, hard, to which the patient does not feel sensation, then do not touch that because it is gangrenous.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then you put the oil in it, and incisions it with a subtle spatula, and incisions it from all parts; and if blood comes to you during the hour of your operation, then you place above the zegi trissat, that is vitriol, within which the blood is restrained.
And then return to your work within which you had treated it with compliment of it, within which it is washed with wine and salt; and cure this with the other cure with water within which it is healed.
Chapter .xxxvi.
Of the extraction of an abscess of the ears and of that which is nothing in the throat and of the other abscesses.
When in the throat it hurts, so are the glands which are assembled with a lump, which sometimes occur outside and are called boils, which when you cure them with water as we have said in the division and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of dark color, of little sensation, then do not touch that with iron.
And if it is of red color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with iron because of the flow of blood; let it rest within which it is matured, and then either you will remove it, or it will burst by itself.
And if it is of white color, soft, and the root of it is subtle, then that is the one that should be incised.
And the operation in it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess of it has already rested with a complete rest or if it is diminished with any diminution; and then do so that the patient stands before the sun, and his head be in the tieu se, and open the mouth of him, and the servant takes between his hands, pressing the tongue of him from below with a noble instrument of which this is the form:
And it is made of silver or of brass, and it is subtle like a knife.
Therefore, as you press with that the tongue and it is shown to you the abscess, and gaze upon that with your eyesight, then take oil and put it in the wound, and pull that to the parts outside as much as you can. Other water that you pull some cause from the tunics, and then incision that with the instrument of which this is the form:
Other water that you pull some cause from the tunics, and then incision that with the instrument of which this is the form: And it is assembled like forceps, however the extremities of it are curved, so that the orifice of each one is in the straight of the other, very sharp, the one which is of iron indi embedded.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then incision that with a spatula of which this is the form of it: And it is sharp on one part, not sharp on the other part.
And since you have incised one well, incision the other according to this same manner of incision, and then the incision the patient wash with warm water
But if there is a flow of blood, wash with water of decoction of the bark of millefolium or of myrtle leaf and similar to those of the stiptic causes within which the flow of blood is restrained, and then cure this within which it is healed.
And sometimes there are born in the throat other abscesses other than those boils, and incision those according to what I have said in the incision of two wells equally.
And I had already treated a woman of an abscess which was located in the throat of her, which abscess had a brownish color, of little feeling, which had already closed the throat of her, and the woman breathed with tight meat, and had already prohibited her from eating and drinking water, and she had already arrived at death if it had not been for a day or two.
And the abscess had already grown so much that it had two openings through which it exited.
Therefore, I treated her fistularly and stayed in one of the openings, and I pulled out that one, and a large portion of it was thrown out of it, and then I incised that one where it understood from the other opening, and then I opened the mouth of it, and I compressed its tongue, and then I stayed the opening in that abscess.
And then I abscised a large portion of it, and it did not flow from it except a little blood. Therefore, the throat of that woman was absolutely closed, and she began to drink water immediately, and then I ate food, so I did not stop inciding that abscess many times for a long time, and abscesses succeeded in place of that one, which incision entered while it was prolonged.
Therefore, I made a needle and cauterized the throat of her, so as to add nothing more; and then I walked around and did not know what God had done to her from then on.
Chapter .xxxvii.
Of incision of a boil when it is abscesses.
When catarr arises in the boil, and it is abscesses and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the part below thick and round, and then it is called a bubo.
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and it does not compare its cure, and it is seen many times that the abscess cauterized is already gone from it and it is subtle, then you should incisions that which is aggregated round; and if it is to that length, and it is bloody, of brownish color or black, and it is not felt in it, then you should attempt the incision of it, because in that there is fear over the patient.
And when you see that according to the manner that I have said of its whiteness and length, that you should make the patient lie down and press the tongue of him with an instrument of which you process its form, and then place the opening in the navel, and pull that down and incise it with one of the two instruments which are said in the incision of two wells.
And you should not incise that one but that which is adjusted over the natural term its addition, because if you incise it more, you will lead to its speaking.
And after the incision, use that which has been recommended in the incision of two wells, and cure that until it is healed.
But if the patient was frightened by the incision of it, then you should use a needle in the cauterization of it its fear, and the manner of the cautery in it is nothing but with sharp water, and it is that you place the head of the patient in your hand, and then press the tongue of him with the instrument which we have said, and then take from the water which we have said in the chapter of cauterization, and compare it with that living cause, and place that one not thick nor subtle, and from that one fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the shape:
Let the extramitat of its concavity in which the medicine is placed be as the concavity of a spoon of ray, and place the instrument with the medicine on the boil, and the patient yawns on his side so that saliva or mucus of the mouth of that one flows through the intrinsic medicine, so that it does not descend from that one for any cause over the throat of him and that it leads to that.
And then with your hand on the medicine, and express that on the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see that one being darkened and sedated by the bite of the medicine.
And if you want, take a cotton and envelop that over the extremity of the ray, and immerse the cotton in the medicine, and introduce the ray with the cotton into a tube of about, until the cotton adheres in that tube, and do this as many times as you can achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then remove that one as it dries and falls about three days or four.
And if you were to say that you repeat the medicine, repeat that one, and after the cauterization abstergeys in the circuit of the uvula with cotton immersed in border, and modify with that which is in the circuit of it of the medicine, and be washed with cold water, and be cured for the moment with embrocation, and dedicate with gargles until it is healed.
And this kind of cure is more saving than incision and more long of fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with that which is more subtle than cauterization and incision, because it is cured with fumigation in this manner: take calamus, hyssop, origan, rue, siccated arabic, chamomile, borage and similar to those of the herbs, and adjust all those causes, or some of them, in an olla, and be submerged with hot water, and boil; and the olla be well washed with decent lut, and be in the middle of the olla a hole, on which be placed an instrument concavity of ram according to this manner; be made of silver or of ram.
And the extremity is introduced into the mouth of the patient until the vapor reaches the uvula above the palate, until the uvula is moved and obscured.
And then iterate over those moved veins until it is dry, and guard yourself that you do not do this cure at the beginning of the emergence of abscesses, because it would too much tighten in the abscess, and in the case that you do this, you must declare the abscess of it caut.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a cane and compose in the extremity of it a hood of wood, so that the mouth of the patient be covered, because the hood of wood prohibits the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good with health.
Chapter .xxxviii.
Of the extraction of thorns and of what adheres in the throat and of the other causes.
Many veins adhere in the throat or a thorn of flesh or any other cause.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extract what of those appears, on which you cast the sight, because you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it is declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear and is to you hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because a strong force the cause annexed the throat is thrown-up by vomit; or that the patient swallows a piece of bread or a root of lettuce or a piece of dry bread; or press a piece of dry sea sponge, and tie it tightly with thread, and then the patient swallows it, because it comes to the place in which the thorns and are extracted, then it is thrown-up. And more, take an eel and tie it with thread, and its end is placed and its tail is extracted; or take a worm and suck its head and suck its tail.
But if it is not thrown-up with those causes which we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form:
And it is thicker than a small ray, and in the extremity of it is concavity which you introduce into its throat with ease.
And hold its head elevated upwards, and guard yourself that the touching of its bone of the throat, so that it is elongated to its body and with that which pushes the bone or the thorn, or that the physician with his hand.

Chapter .xxxix.
Of the extraction of bloodsucker annexed to the throat.
When it is medicated bloodsucker with what we have said in the division of the cure with medicines and does not confer, consider then in the throat of the patient to the sun, because you have compressed the tongue of it with the instrument which to you we have recommended.
Therefore, if you see the bloodsucker, extract it with a small pin or with a subtle gesture decently.
And if it is not to you possible to extract with that, then take a concave cannula and introduce that into the throat of the patient, and then place in the concavity of the cannula a burning iron, and do this as many times (until the bloodsucker is dead and is removed from the throat) and abstain the patient all day of water.
And then press a vessel full of cold water, and work the mouth of it in that, and wash the mouth with that; and do not swallow of that any cause and evil the water one hour after another with its hand, because the bloodsucker as soon as it feels the water catz.

Do this, so in that it falls.
And it is another way of operation in the subfumigation of that, is that you take a bowl in which there are burning embers and the bowl is covered with a cover in the middle of which there is a hole, and it is composed in that hole the extremity of the instrument; and then let go downwards the subfumigation; and place the patient his mouth at the extremity of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the subfumigation is not thrown-up until you know that the subfumigation has arrived at the bloodsucker, because the bloodsucker as soon as it falls.
And if it does not catch, then you iterate the subfumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eat causes salty and alhs, and do not drink water, and thus necessary is that with this regimen it be thrown-up.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the bloodsucker of the throat is treated when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps as you see, except that in it there is some curvature: which is placed in the throat, and the extremity of it is similar to the beak of a stork, which is gaunt, in which it is sharpened with a file, so that, when compressing any cause, that does not leave it.
Chapter .xl.
In which is the sum of the sermon in the opening of abscesses and in their section.
Abscesses are special sores, of many kinds, according to what we remember of them, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and section by two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses in themselves; and by what we understand of the humidities.
And the second way is according to the places where they occur in the body, for the abscess that occurs in the head is different from the abscess that occurs in the buttock; and the abscess in the fleshy place is different from the abscess that occurs in the joint; and to each of them is its own treatment by operation.
And more than abscesses there are some that ought not to be perforated but rather when the pus is ripe in them or the completion of them.
There are other abscesses that ought to be drained when they are ripe, and not when they are fully matured according to their completion, just as abscesses that are near the joints, for when an abscess occurs near a joint, its being is prolonged until it can be drained, and then it is often corrupted the ligaments or nerves of that joint, so that it causes contraction of that limb; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay the perforation of it until it is fully ripe, it will spread to the principal member; or it is near the buttock, therefore perforate it before it is ripe; and you ought not to perforate it crude, of non-completed digestion, but the one that is near the buttock, so that you can prevent the protrusion of it and its elongation to the parts behind the buttock, and a fistula is made, or that it is done in the term of it that it is not healed. But more than that, you ought to know the hour of the perforation of the abscess, and thus it is when it is ripe.
And you ought to know that it is ripe when there is the cessation of pain and the cessation of fever, and according to the diminution of the heat and of the pulse, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And that you ought to know the perforation to be done in the place pus after the abscess and pus coming from the cure of the eminence.
And the perforation always be done along the length of the flesh or of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the arms or of the feet, and in the places of the tendons, of the nerves, of the arteries; and, lastly, in all places equal in which there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use of perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you let the perforation of it until it is confirmed the digestion of it according to completion, as we have said; for if you perforate that before it is ripe, it is prolonged the course of the pus of it and of much filth, and often the lips of it are hardened and the depth of it.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or of the body, according to the necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And you ought to administer a small perforation, and in large abscesses a large perforation and many sections according to the diversity of the greatness of the abscesses.
And others are of abscesses in which the incision ought to be concave and be incised, when it is done so that bread is made and it is done in a term that it already follows, just as the one that occurs in trops of boils and buboes.
And of those are those that are incised with a section having three angles; and of those is the one in which it is incised as the figure of a myrtle leaf, just as the abscesses of the angles.
And of them are those in which a round incision and a lunar incision, and similar to those are administered.
But that one in which there is no such as their abscesses are superficial, you ought to perforate that with a superficial perforation, or flat only.
And you ought when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, that you perforate it and do not take that you extract all the pus in that hour; have extracted from it one part, and then close the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger part, and then do this many times according to gradation until it is completely drained.
And especially if the patient is of a weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old person in decrepitude, for the animal spirit many times is resolved with the egress of the pus, and often the patient dies, and you operate on them.
And guard this chapter with great care, and in the way you perforate these abscesses, you ought to abstain from the plagues and consider therefore if the plague of the abscess is few, or is a flat section, then you administer the poultices of it or of a counter-poultice; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you ought to put a piece of cloth in each tract until they come together.
And if in the abscess is incised a part of the incision or has concaved it in the circuit, then you ought to cover it with old cloth or with a thread made of linen of it, as when you have treated poultices or dressings, its moisture; and strengthen that in the third day; and then process this and cure it with what is due of the dressings until it is healed.
But if blood flows during the operation, then you administer cold water and vinegar to which you have placed a cloth, and place that over the area of the blood flow many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer regimen and cure as I have recommended in many places in this book and elsewhere.
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you must enfund pulvils in wine and oil caut, and place those on the area.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you must place poultices soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all that be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you must open the abscess and cleanse it and administer in its care what is appropriate until it is healed.
And thus is what is necessary of the knowledge of the cure of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already said of all abscesses in what manner is the way of its cure by chapters individually.

Chapter XLIV.
Of the incision over the abscess which happens in the throat outside, and is called a hernia of the throat.

Chapter XLV.
Of the incision of the species of nodes.
Of nodes there are many species and I have already said all the species of them in the division.
And I want to recount thus the difference between a node and an excrescence as if there were doubt.
Therefore I say that in an excrescence there is heat and fever and violent pain until the boiling of the superfluity subsides; and it is complicated by putrefaction that at that time the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But in a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that hard lump which is in the sac properly.
And it is according to the color of the body, and the beginning of it is like a cheese and is made so as a vessel, whether it is larger or smaller, and they are according to two species, whether they are fatty and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the cure of a node, that you explore it and inquire about it at the beginning with the instrument which is called an intromes, according to what will come its form in the chapter which is next to this.
And the manner of inquiry of abscesses and of all nodes, and it is that you take this instrument and introduce it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the core.
And then finally your hand according to the measure of the greatness of the abscess, and you extract the intromes and consider what is emitted in the trace of it.
And if the humidity is flowing out, of whatever color it may be, then you incise it with a superficial incision according to what I have said in the other abscesses.
And if from the trace of the intromes there is no humidity, then you know that it is fatty.
And cut over it with an incision having the form of a cross according to this form: as you have been taught, and suspend it with tweezers, and excise the core from every part with ease, and guard the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that pus with the node.
But if it is broken in the operation, it is not possible to extract that pus, and many times it is necessary to do so, and then you extract it with pieces until there is no cause of it remaining.
For if from it there remains any cause, little or much, the node returns according to the most.
And if you see that from it remains any small part, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and strengthen the wound, and place on top of it a cautery and cure it with the other cure until it is healed.
And if it is a large node, then you sew the two lips of it and cure it with what is necessary for healing.
But if a pulsating vein is against it, or not pulsating, then you cut and cauterize the place of zig-zag that is vitriol and leave that straight for two or three days until you can stitch the wound and that the boiling of the blood has subsided; and then return to incise what is broken from the node.

Chapter .xlix.
Of the section of abscesses that occur because of an artery or vein.
When an artery is ruptured, and the groove that is above it is consolidated, then many times abscesses occur. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are by the artery or vein: because when the abscess is by artery it is long aggregated in the front of the body; and that when you pierce the abscess with your fingers, you feel in it a kind of stridor.
And that which is by vein is red around the manifest of the body.
And the section on this abscess is an error, and most of all in that which is from the armpits, and in the corners, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore it is necessary that its cure with iron be avoided, and that which is in the extremities, or in the head, it must be avoided.
But on that which is of them by inflammation of the orifice of the artery, divide in the groove a long section; and then open the section with hooks and then the artery, and deliver from the tunics until it is uncovered.
And then put on that needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and tighten the artery with a double thread in two places, — which cause you to see in the extraction of two arteries which are in the temples.
And then prick the place which is between the two ligaments with a spatula until there is all the blood that is in it, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer a cure in which it is engendered power until the ligaments fall; and take care of it with convenient washes to water until it is healed.
And if the abscess is by the section of a vein, then it is necessary that you have with your hand that which is possible of the abscess with the groove, and then introduce the needle from the place which you have had from your hand, and make it penetrate; and be in that double thread until you extract that from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a good ligament according to what I have told you in the ligament of the grape of two parts with two extremities of the thread.

Then dry the abscess in the middle place of it until when it is issued that which is in it, then you cut the superfluity of the groove, and leave that which of it is tied.
And then place on it the powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the cure which is made with poultices and washes until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens by twisting of nerves.
Thus as abscesses happen in arteries and veins, and similarly abscesses happen in the nerves when they are struck or happen by excessive work, and by similar means.
And it is made the most in the places of the auxiliary bones or the talus, or in every place where the joints are bad.
And it is a serious abscess, and it is assembled to the color of the other body, and in the major of its dispositions it is of its own and when it is tightened with violence, it feels in the patient a semblance of stupor.
And it is not abscesses joined in the front of the body, and for this it is left the chain, and it is bad to all parts and does not go to the parts ahead or behind.
Therefore those that are in the joints should not be filled with iron, because very quickly they will become inflamed.
But the one that is in the head or in the front, end the chain with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then take it with the forceps and cut it from the roots.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with an anchor, and excoriate it, and then close it; and adjust the plate with suture, and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter .li.
Of the section of abscess which happens from a boil that happens to the belly.
And it happens to some men growths in the bellies of those and in other parts of the body of those, the ones that are called fungals for similarity to those with fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head of it is thick; and already the lips of those are converses, and it is of those that is small, and it is of those that is very magnified.

Chapter .lxix.
Of castration.
Know that castration in our law is exempted, but for the reason it is brought to my mind in this book; and it is not necessary to remember it except for two ways, of which one is that it be in the knowledge of the physician when he is asked about it, that he knows the cure of it to whom it happens; and the other way is that we many times have need of castration of other animals for our benefit from them, as well as from goats, and sheep, and pigs, and similar to the animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by attrition, or by fissure to section.
But in that which is done by attrition, the way of the operation is that we make the beast to be in water tail while its genitals are softened and are limp and that they hang. And then take those with your hand while they are resolved and do not appear after the touch.
But in castration done by fissure and section, cover that you have the beast and press the cord of the testicles of it with your left hand.

And then cut those that you will have each excoriated. And do not leave on those of the head any cause, but the subtle head which is above the vessels.
And this way of castration is better the one done by attrition, because attrition fort leaves no cause of the testicles for which the beast desires coyt.
And then cure the plague while it is healed.

Chapter LXXII.

And if the midwife is skillful and does what is done with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is ingenious according to every figure according to what is prepared for her by the ingenious one leading to health.
A remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes things are formed in the womb one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten, and it is already known to me that one woman aborted six, and another one ten, all formed, the cause was made by measurement of the glorious wise.
