Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you cure the dead fetus with what we have said in the division of the medicine and it is not expelled outside, and you are forced to use operation with your hand, then it is necessary to consider if the woman is of sound virtue and is not among those accidents by which she is feared to be harmed.
Then it is necessary that the woman be placed on her back over her two, and her head be inclined downwards and her thighs be elevated.
And the minister shall have those two parts or be tied in the bed so that the body of him does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mouth of the mother with oily and with mucilage of althea and fennel and seed of lily.
And then the midwife anoints her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then she inserts her hand with facility into the mother, and then she seeks with her hand in the place where the forceps are in the fetus.
And convenient place in this: therefore you consider if the fetus is prepared that descends on its head, and then you place the forceps in the heels of it, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or in the palate, or under the beard, or in the fork of it, or in the proper places of the back, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is on the feet of it, then in the penile, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary that you have the forceps with your right hand, and pause the curvature of it in that place which is between the fingers of your left hand, and insert your hand with facility and place the forceps in one of those places which we have said, until you reach the left cause.
And then pause and place in the opposite position of it another forceps, or the third if it is necessary, so that the traction be equal, and not be extension according to its straightness alone.
And may it be good to all parties, so that the expulsion of it be alleviated as it is done during the eradication of it.
And it is necessary in it that which is between them the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoints some of her fingers with oil and inserts them skillfully and revolves with those that are retained.
Therefore if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the forceps shall be changed into the place which is more out; and do so until the whole fetus is expelled.
But if the hand is outside the other, and it is not possible to reduce it by the constriction of it, then it is necessary that a cloth be enveloped over it; and then extract it until the whole is expelled, and incisions it either in the junction of the hand or in the groin.
And so it is necessary that you do in the other hand and in the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is an accident to it constriction in the expulsion, or it is in the head of it water adjusted, then it is necessary that you insert between the fingers the spatula spine and split the head and extract the water, or what disrupts it with an instrument which is named "alundat", the form of which will come with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is next.
And similarly do if the fetus is of a large head by nature: and then it is necessary that you incision it with the algematu, or that you press it with the alunda as we have said; and then extract the bones with the forceps.
And if the head is outside and is constricted by the fork, then it is split until the moisture which is in the feet is poured out.
But if it is not complicated, nor obeys to expulsion, then you incision the head as soon as it is possible to you.
And if the remains of the womb are inflamed or there is hydropsy in it, then it is necessary that you perforate it until the moisture which is in it flows out.
But if the problem is on his feet, then the traction of it is lighter and the direction of it to the mouth of the mother is lighter.
But if it is constricted of the belly and the feet, then cover what you extract with the cloth which you will have enveloped over your hand, and make the belly and the feet inside that it may run what is in those.
But if the other limbs are stayed and the head is turned, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mouth of the mother is obstructed, then she inserts her hand into the perineum of the latter, and with that she seeks the head, and pulls it into the mouth of the mother, and then inserts a hook, or two hooks, and pulls it with those.
And if the mouth of the mother is obstructed by an abscess that occurs to the one, then cover that it not be trebled upon it; cover that then administer effusions of humid fatty causes. And administer plasters; and follow the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the problem is on his side, then if possible, be pricked; and be administered the water which we have said in the living fetus.
And if that is not possible, then be incised in the buttocks, and be extracted.
And cover that you leave no cause of the second.
And when you have completed your whole operation, then administer the water which we have said of the cure of the abscesses that occur from the mother.
And if there occurs a flux of blood, then descend into styptic waters and administer the other cure.
And I have already seen a woman in whose belly was a dead fetus, and about that fire another pregnancy, and about fire dead is the fetus other. It happened to one about a long time ago an abscess in his navel; and it inflated until fire opened, and began to throw out pus.
And I was called to the cure of him, and medicated him for a long time, and the plague was not consolidated.
Then I put on him some causes of strong attraction plasters, and extracted from him a bone, and about a few days passed, and extracted another bone; and I was too amazed, as in the belly there were no bones.
And then I thought that they were of the bones of the dead fetus.
Therefore I pursued the plague, and extracted from him many bones, the woman existing in her thousands dispositions, and already living in such a manner for a long time, cured of him a little pus.
And I do not affirm that this is strange, but that in this there is science and help to what the doctor artifices with his hand for the cure administers.

Of the extraction of the second.
When the second is retained during childbirth, then it is necessary that you command the patient to retain her breath, and then make that stir with the condition, and strengthen your hand over its mouth and over its nostrils, and if by this it is expelled outside, well is.
And if not, then you take a cloth and perforate in the covering a hole and place in that herbs burning the mouth of the mother, thus as calament and rue and aneth and chamomile and siccated arabic and cassia lignea and santaurea. All these herbs or some of them are submerged in water in the cloth, and place that over the fire.
And then place a tube of cane in the mouth of the mother, and keep that inside so that the vapor comes to the cavity of the mother.
And then make that stir as we have said, and the second is expelled with velocity.
But if remaining after this cure and it is not expelled, then you command the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or in a mixture of althea, and then introduce that into the vagina and search with that the second, and as that is found, then take it and extend it little by little. But if it is annexed in the perineum of the mother, then introduce her hand as we have recounted, and when she will have found the second, extract it little by little according to rectum so that the mother does not feel the traction vehement. It is necessary that it be changed to the right and left sides with ease.
And if the mouth of the mother is constricted, we have already recounted the cure with stirrup and with medication of the cloth with herbs.
But if it is not expelled with all the causes which we have recounted, then keep that you do not tire on it in iteration of abstraction, but rather that what is emitted from it to the mouth of the mother lies to the cushion of the woman, and then sterilize that with ointment and tetrafarmacon; because that will make it soften and after a few days it will be dissolved and expelled.
But when it can be done, it is elevated from it bad odor to the head, and to the stomach, and this is what is done to the patient; and therefore it is necessary that fumigations and conveniences be administered to it.
But some of the Ancients already are praised fumigations of nazitort and of dried figs.
Shape of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and of the second:
It is made of wood or of brass.
And it is placed the subtle extremity in the vagina and the wide extremity over the fire, and the fumigation is placed over the embers of the fire.
And keep that inside so that the fumigation is consumed and repeat another.
Of cure of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born and the anuses of them are not perforated, the ones which already have a subtle and opaque. Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to perforate with her finger that opaque; and if not, perforate it with a sharp spatula, and keep that it does not touch the rectum.
And then place over that a cloth infused in wine and oil; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is opaque, then place in the hole a tube for many days, and remove it when the child will want to defecate.
And already happens opacity of the anus by consolidation of plague or of abscess; therefore it is necessary that that consolidation be incised, and then be cured with what we have said of the cure and of the lead.
Of cure of the fistulas that originate from the anus.
The fistulas that originate in the part of the anus lower than the nodulation and thickness of which originate near the anus outside or in the space of the one part.
And it is a fistula or many.
And as that nodulation is ancient and runs from the humid watery white or subtle pockmarking, and some times from these fistulas some penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by that which is emitted from them by the egress and by windiness; and sometimes the patient of egress, and for a long time it is emitted from them worms.
And some of those fistulas sometimes penetrate into the space, into the bladder, and into the meat of the vagina; and some of these penetrate into the junction of the vagina and into the knot of the buttock.
And in which it is known that a fistula penetrates into the anus and does not penetrate, it is that you put your finger inside the anus, and you introduce a subtle probe of thread or iron into the fistula when there is no turbidity.
But if there is turbidity in that, then introduce a subtle probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor do you perceive any cause of evacuation or worm or wind, thus as we have said, then you know that it is not penetrating, and then you turn to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the meat of the vagina, then the meaning of it is the emission of urine from that and prohibition of it so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the junction of the vagina or into the buttock and causing pain in the circuit, then the sign of it is that the probe enters here if there is no turbidity in it, into the buttock, and causing pain in the circuit of the part; and that from it there is always pus; and that it does not benefit from that medicine, nor is it consolidated with an enema.
And in all those fistulas penetrating there is no cure of all, and the cure of those is the work and vanity of the leaves of the physicians upon which it is founded.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected according to what I have told you, and what I have already proved.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on their back and elevates their legs into them, the thighs being inclined to their belly, and then introduce a probe of lead, or iron if there is turbidity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if that feels the patient around the buttock, then cover that you introduce your finger inside the buttock.
And if you feel in your finger the probe already by itself being inserted, or another thing that you feel between your finger and that skin or flesh, then you know certainly that that is penetrating; and therefore do not work on that, for it is not in that healing, as we have said.
But however, some say that it is healed in some men tart.
And of the medicine by which is expected benefit in it is that you ignite a subtle cautery according to the size of the fistula, as it is preceded, and introduce that ignited into the fistula until it reaches around the buttock; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those flesh adjusted to the fistula, which are like the quill of a bird's feather, are already cauterized.
And then cure that with poultices enveloped in border until all those flesh which are cauterized can be covered, and then cure that with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, it is well; and if not, then it is not healed in any time by other cure.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate into your finger which is in the buttock, and between that and the probe which is veiled with flesh or skin, and sometimes the fistula in that which seizes the surface of the pudenda, then dry the pudenda from the beginning of the fistula, and you see drying with the probe, and it is in the fistula until the drying reaches the place where the extremity of the probe reaches, and by the probe it is liberated and catched.
And then purify those fistula flesh which are assembled to the quill of a bird's feather and do not break from those any cause.
But if a flux of blood sometimes comes between you and your operation when you are inserting those flesh, then the best that it may be and of the light pus instrument is cauterized with fire or with acute medicine, for cauterizing with fire adjusts good two habits: it is the cauterization of those flesh adjusted, and abscessing of the blood excretion of the humidities.
And then cure that with poultices enveloped in border or in caus trissat until the place gets brace, and then cure the plague with enemas flesh hard generating and consolidating.
And so medicines in which there is styptic and excretion; when then it is restored already healed and you do not fear iteration.
But if the fistula already reaches into the perineum of the buttock and is elongated from that surface of the body, then introduce your finger into the buttock and inquire with that.
And therefore if you feel the probe and between you and it is veiled with skin or flesh, and is proper to penetration, then it is not operation in it but according to your trust and hope.
The which cause is to administer in it one of three ways: or that cauterize it as we have said; or that dry it until you achieve the concavity of it so that it is possible for you to introduce the probes and the proper cure; and do not proceed to cut by so that you do not wound the lacert containing the buttock, and that happens upon the patient's emission of feces by their will; and then cure it with what we have said that is strong cure; or that elongate that veil and cleanse that pus that dries it within the buttock, as we have said, or with probe or another instrument of acute extremity, and then purify those fistula flesh which are in the section where you can with percrustation; and then strive to make the restoration of the whole plague with section, and if the orifice of pus above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the buttock, and pus light from the patient. And in some cases, the fistula is ended in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the latz of the anus around the surface of the body with the codena and on the edge of the anus, then take the probe of perforated extremity, which is like an awl of preparing the hearts according to this form:

And then cure the plague until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the orifice of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way it is prepared for you, so that it is not moved in the groin, for it is not for you to do this so that you do not injure the groin; and then make it with that until it is healed.

Of suture of hemorrhoids in which blood runs in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are in two ways, for they are between the anus like those that are of coals, or those that are like the fat of a rasim. And of those are small and large, and that the blood runs from those always and it is one, and they are many, and they are outside the anus, and in the extremities of it.
But however, those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, of which water citrina or a little blood with continuous body runs, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the cure of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to defecate and to express himself while the anus is empty and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you take it and suspend with hooks or what you have those with your nail, and then you incise those of their roots.
But if the hooks are not held in those because of the humidity of them and their softening, then take those with rough cloth or with a stick or with wool fleece, and extract those with your fingers, and then incise those and pulverize over them some powdered caudas near the incision, so that they are in those place of cauterization; or cauterize those according to what is preceded in the chapter of cauterization. And then cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey the egress, then christenize the patient with a cristeri in which there is a small bite, and wash it with that water that is in the anus and that obeys the egress lightly when the patient expresses himself.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause of them is lighter, and it is that you take those with your nail, or that you take those with a hook, and incise those, and then cure those with the water that we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, cover that you administer suture of them according to this way: take a faithful enveloped and insert it into a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle into the root of the other part, and envelope the two extremities of the faithful in the part farther from the needle, and it is laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with strong structure, and then untie the faithful and extract the needle.
And do this to all, and leave one of those untied, so that the superfluity of the blood runs from that one.
And then place on the anus cloth soaked in rose oil or in cerot; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave that until it dries; and when it is dried, cure those with ointments or with other cure until it is healed.
And as they are old and are not of use in that medicine, then you must cut those with the point of the spatum or with your nail until they are made humid and there is a host of those the pus that flows from below, which prohibits those from consolidation; and then cure those until they are consolidated.
Then you must cut those with a reason pus vehement that the first one until it is bloody and they are excoriated; and then cure those until they are healed.
Know that alamermar, it is called, and it is not if not a knot round according to the color of the body resembling the head of a clavicle, and it is made in all the body and mostly in the parts below the feet and the fingers, and therefore by those pains the ailment.
And therefore, whoever is cut in that which is in the circle of the key, and is held with the forceps or an instrument, and is eradicated from its root; and then care for that.

More of a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round eminence on the surface of the body, and often times they appear on the hands of children.
And the cure for it is that you extend and cut that, or that you tighten that with a thread or with a needle that is sewn into the skin. And if you want, cauterize that, according to what we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
But a stye is a small,  feathered or fistulous, thick eminence on the surface of the body, going into the preon, and when the  flegm  finds that, it is felt in it resembling a bite of an ant, and it is made around the body.
And the cure for it is that you take a needle of swan feather or of vulture feather, and it is strong, and you place that on the stye inside that it contains the circle of the needle of the parts of it; and then you revolve your hand with the needle inside that you cut that which is in the circle of the stye, and you place that in the preon of the flesh, and that you eradicate the stye from its root.

Of the cure of the plagues.
And I have already said in the division of the cure of the plagues that which was convenient to the book, and I say so the cure of those with my hand and with medicines according to the complement.
Therefore I say that the plagues are diversified according to what in which they are made the plagues and according to the place upon which befall the plagues.
And the causes for which many times are made the plagues so as by the  bite of a serpent, or incision of a spear or of a sword, or percussion with a lance, or with clubs, or with arrows, or similar to those of many causes.
May the plagues that are in the places of the body be like the plague that catches on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the figure, and similar to those of the limbs.
And you are counting the cure of some plagues so that you pause that reasoning and rule over the other plagues.
And you begin with the plagues of the head simple, because the remembrance of the composed will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is due to a stone or similar, and the bone has been broken only a little and it is a great plague, and you fear about that patient having a boil, then you take care and immediately perform a cephalic phlebotomy, and do not delay this; and extract from that blood according to the quantity of its virtue, if no other cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that is drawn from the plague is little.
And it is phlebotomy on the opposite side of the afflicted area; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if any cause prohibits phlebotomy, then reduce the food and do not approach meat or wine.
And put on the plague, if a boil occurs, a cloth infused in rose oil or in wine in which is stippled.
But if it is certain of a boil, then put on the plague, if it is recent with its blood and the air has not changed that powder which I will recommend soon.
And if the air has already changed that, then put on poultices until it is healed.
But if the plague is large or is due to an incision of the skin and similar things, and the lips of it are not adjusted by sutures, then adjust those with sutures according to how I will recommend of the sutures of them.
But if the plague is already discovered from the bone and flesh, and it is not a retention of it but a small suspension, then incise that and treat the plague with medicines and restore the flesh in the place of the bone, and command the patient to be frugal of foods in which there is thickness: caps and forms, and similar to them, until it is composed.
And if a putrefaction arises in the bone and does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that and throw it away, and then treat that.
But if in these plagues an artery or vein is found from which blood can flow, and it is not cut off by medicines, then you inquire about the artery, and if you find that not incised, then you incise that with a spatula, or cauterize it; and if necessity compels you that what we have said does not confer, then you cauterize it until the blood is cut off.
And if these plagues are simple and small, then the cause of them is light; so that you pulverize on those this powder, because it consolidates before the air changes them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take olive oil, one part, dragon's blood, two parts, and non-extinguished cautery, three parts; let all be ground finely and sifted, and let the plague be filled with it, and be constricted by a good constriction until it is well adhered, and coagulated with the blood.
And after strengthening that with poultices and leaving it, do not undo it while it is like that so that it is not suppurated, because the medicine is not extracted from it until it is healed.
And sometimes do this alone with cautery, when you do not have present olive oil and dragon's blood.
And already do this same operation in the great plagues when it is necessary that it be placed on them and be constricted, and are recent plagues with their blood, those which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is in summer, then it is not understood in the position of this powder, but rather treat those with what we have said, so that you place on them some digested poultices, or that you place on them powder of barley flour with water and honey until they get a poultice; and then treat those with the other cure until they are healed.
But if a plague occurs with a fracture in the bone, and it is small, then you extract that with a spatula.
And I say the cure of the fractures of the head in that which is venomous.
Of the plagues of the neck.
But if the plague occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication, and between the plague of the head when it is simple.
But if the nerve of the neck is incised or the artery, then if it is a nerve, do not treat it, but rather that its cure be avoided with what makes vehement styptication, such as ziniar and zegi that is vitriol and similar to them, because they are harmful to the nerves and oppose that. And do not place on the plague any cause of all cold, because the substance of the nerves is cold and the continuation of it is with the brain, which is the most delicate of all the members.

Of the cure of wounds and the quality of clysterization with medicines.

Of cupping and the qualities of using those.
Cupping is of horns, or of earthenware, or of copper.
And cupping is done according to two manners: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping that is without scarification is according to two manners, because it is with fire or without fire.
And to the cuppings that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood there are fourteen places of the body, of which one in which cupping is placed is alnocrati, and it is the rear part of the head; and the middle place of the two parts of the neck together; and cupping of the chin below the jaw more below the mouth; and cupping of two spatulas; and cupping of alohoster, and it is on the end of the shoulder; and cupping of two focils, and it is of two arms; and cupping of two groins; and cupping of two thighs.
And ventosation does not extract the blood but from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not catch the virtue through those, just as it is caught by phlebotomy; nor is it right that ventosation be done with fire or with fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity provokes ventosation for illness or for continuous, administer it at every hour at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and in any time it is.
And therefore when times in any cause of those, and properly if it is in the third or middle of the month, then command ventosation to be done because it has passed from the day of two or three hours.
And the benefit of ventosation of the neck is that when that confers the gravity to the head and to what is poured into the eyes; but it is right that this is after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this ventosation is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic.
And it is not light that use of it that one who is of frequent congestion, or in which is catarrh, because it gives to that great harm.
And therefore it is not right that use of that the old, and in the heads of whom are frequent illnesses.
But to that one who over that is continuing over it is done oblivion; and therefore it is right that you command the ventosator that pause his hand a little with the ventosa to the parts below, fearing the generation of oblivion.
But ventosation of the ankle is in the place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and basilica: and therefore confers on asthma, and on the constriction of the lung and on the fissure of the instrument of the lung, and on cough, and on repletion.
And it is right that the ventosation of the ankle be expelled a little, because if it is paused below, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And ventosation of the alhagdahan confers on the pains accidental in the head and on ophthalmia, and migraine, and pre-focus, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in the place of phlebotomy of the basilica.
And it is right that you command the ventosator that he not press his hand with scarification so that it does not pierce the artery and occur fluxes of blood.
And ventosation below the chin confers on alcohol in the mouth, and on corruption of the gums, and appearances to those of the illnesses that are in the mouth, and stand in the place of phlebotomy of aliheric that is in the two lips.
But the ventosation that is done on the two spatulas confers on caphatan, which is made by repletion of heat.
And the ventosation of the two parts of the teeth of the focils confers benefit of phlebotomy of three veins, so it is of the basilica, fusca and cephalica, because that one attracts the blood of all those veins more numerous that are in the flesh, and those veins smaller of the other veins more thick until it reaches the attraction to the three veins.
And command the ventosator that too much he does not proceed in scarification, because the place is denuded of flesh, and below it are nerves and arteries.
And it is a ventosation with which is ventosated over those and confers on the hemorrhoids of the anus and on the ulcers more below.

Chapter Second: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and their causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by the blow of a weapon, and the blow breaks all the bones within the skull, which is left the bone, as if it were done in the shaft, and for this reason this species is called ascial; or the weapon incides some part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision into the rear of it; and this species of fracture is called absolute levitation.
And the plague of these two fractures or is great or small, and of fracture that is contortion or attrition; and the cause of it is percussion with a hammer or a fall on a rock, and their likenesses.
And this fracture or is penetrating that already approaches the panniculus which is left the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures or is great or small.
And of fracture is that which is entwined in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair, and for this reason this species is called capillary fracture.
And is fracture that is made either by a fall or by percussion of a rock and its likenesses, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter into the parts within, and it is made in the concave place as if it happened to the payroles of a ram when it happens to their percussion and it is part of their entrance into the parts within.
And most of all this is done in the bodies of wet bones, as if they were the heads of infants.
And to all these species of fractures, satagias, they are heavy as if stars separated. And you will come with recollection of all this in its place.
And we shall know all these species of fracture by detection upon those and by inquiry of them with tents and ostentation of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary species is known because it is discovered upon the bone, and it is abstergit, and lined upon it with a tent, because the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fracture, consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification upon pain, as it is vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the joints and redness of those, and likenesses to them in the greatest dispositions, then adopt that it is not healed.
And if you see non-current accidents, you hope for health, then you begin the cure of him.
The cause is that if it has come to you the afflicted in the beginning as it is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you must study in the ostentation of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must study to see the bone before the seventh day, because that which is left the bone be corrupted from the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted may happen.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and shredded, then cover the small and rubbed piece, as I will tell you.
And you raise the patient's head, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the figure of the plague, and that it be light on the patient.
And if it happens to you to see the bone, blood or pus flows, then you oppose it with what you cover, and that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rubbed oil, and make the patient rest until the pus is settled, and if you are sure of the blood flow; and then begin in the circuit of the bone and in its removal.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you cut the bone with a straight incisor like this, and this is the shape of it: And then administer another incisor this one, a little wider than that; and this is the shape of it:
And then administer the wider incisor than the second.
And cover that you have to have a number of different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are at the end of the sharpness of their extremities, and are of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisor so that it does not hit the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then cover that you perforate in its circuit before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-deep drills.
And they are not called thus but because those do not pause the term of the bone of the test to what is about it, for as the drill is a round extremity or margin on which the head of it is left sharp like a margin or a small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a large number of which as many as convenient to the thickness of each bone,  and that it be to you present in all tests of the brain the extremity of which be sharp, the quantity in length and brevity be according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill on the bone and revolve it with your fingers until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and pause it that is between all the tracks the quantity of the thickness of the ray or about that.
And then cut with incisors that which is between each tract of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can on it in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared for it, as such as tongs and subtle forceps.
And cover that you guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any cause of the scalp.
And therefore as you will have circuited the bone, and if it is continued with it, and you will have that extracted, cover that you raise and smooth that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but however cover that it be more thin and more subtle than the others incisors.
And if any pieces of the small bones and splinters remain, then take that with ease with that which you owe you of the instruments; and then take care of the patient with liniments and ointments which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, farther from pain, which says Gualia and the lauzec by great lausor; and this is the sermon of it: it is necessary to you at the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, until when you will have discovered that part and you will have placed below it the extremity of this incisor lent and light of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is long and do not cut any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides forward in length, as sometimes; that the lenticular part be adjusted to the scalp with the face of it incident acute in the bone.
And then strike on the incisor of a part with a small mallet until you cut all the bone with ease as it is revolved, and you be sure of the membrane that no accident happens in it in any manner, and if the medicant14 was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were asleep.

And as you administer in your reason with which roots the bones at the beginning the pus of those roots, and then use the most subtle of it, and do not cease to do so continuously until you reach to administer the pus thick and the most subtle of all those.
In any of the other fractures, small and subtle, as you administer care according to what you perceive for the rectification of it.
And it is because it is not known about all those who have studied in that art. And this is what we have shown written about great fractures.
And if the periosteum is cut when you set the bones, then take cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge it in hot oil, and place it over the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and hot oil and place it over the first cloth and do this as lightly as you can so that you do not injure the scalp; and then administer over this bandage, and do not tighten it except for the quantity that the wound alone has.

And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and not be in it dirt of rinsing nor other; nor let virulence be added to it; for when it is adjusted over the periosteum of the brain, it corrupts that and you can spoil it, and it happens to the patient according to that evil malice.

Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not broken of the nose but two parts together, or one of those sometimes, for in it you have two bones, for the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and it does not happen to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.

Chapter IV.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.

Chapter V.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three manners.

Chapter XI.
Of the Restoration of the Fracture of the Adductor.
Adductor is that which is between the hip to the top of the shoulder.

Chapter .xii.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
Arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and that is the one that sits the little finger, and the other is large and is the one that is placed below the little finger, from the part further down.
And often the major focil is broken alone, or the small one, and often they are broken together.
And when the small focil is broken above, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when the focil of it is broken below the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when the two bones are broken together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the small focil above, then it is convenient to the doctor, must the restoration of it, that pause little extension with facility until that be evened.
But if the large focil is that which is broken, then it must pause extension more vehement.

And then the doctor evens the bones while reducing that according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then you pierce to reduce all pieces in their place with your virtue.
And if there appears in it a splinter separated that pierces the code and is not to your hope in the restoration of it, then you finish over it and oust that according to the manner which we have said above.

And the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture should be soft and moist cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is tightened should be of its own proper middle between fine and coarse, as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and the ligament for a few days. And if any cause occurs for which rectification is necessary, as if the swelling occurs in the limb, then you must immerse the limb in warm water until the swelling ceases.
And let the limb not be constrained until it rests, and then return the bandage.
And if the bandage is already softened and the bone is already set and seems like that, then rectify all of it with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb by excess of bandage, for then you must soften that a little and let it for a few days until nourishment runs to it, and then tighten it.

Chapter XIII.

Chapter XIV.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the bone of the hip.
The bone of the hip is rarely broken and is declared to the feeling according to how it is converted to the front part and to the most sovereign part.

And then the minister extends all the ligament to its part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is proper to the root of the hip, then the ligament is proper to the thigh at the part of the hip, so that the extension comes on top, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is proper to the thigh, then the ligament is made proper to the thigh, and the extension is to the parts below.
And then the physician aligns the bones with both of their hands until it is reduced according to the natural resemblance and the bone is joined with good concavity; then you must place plaster and bandage, unless there is a boil in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in him, then you leave that for a few days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your cure.
The stricture of him is made thus: so that a hard bandage is wrapped over the fracture, and that is folded two or three times, and the superfluity is removed; and then the leg is wrapped up until it reaches the bone and the root of the thigh.
And introduce a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the sides, and the ends of the splint come from the sovereign side of the two parts, and then roll over the leg and thigh what superfluity of the bandage.
And then place on the thigh in the place of that fracture splints, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the stricture is coagulated.
And then begin with a strong splint on the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and the more you move away with the splint from the place of the fracture, the less your splint is and softer and more moist.
And then extend the two ends of the splint to the sides until it reaches the heel of the foot, and strengthen both ends of the splints on the other side so that the splint does not be removed from its place.
And then leave the splint on him, so that no corrosion or abscess or inflammation or similar things occur to the limb; and if any of those things occur, then cut the splint from him, and correct what has occurred again, as we have said many times.
But if in the bone is heavy the bone which you put, then you must straighten it as much as is possible; and if not, then break over it and remove that, and cure with that of which the remembrance precedes until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with other things which are adjusted to it splints, as we have said in the auxiliary and in the arm.
But however in the restoration of him in such a manner made, do not occur to the patient lameness; and if it is restored alone, then with other things which are applied to the other leg, then it is necessary that he limp from the one which is always.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in 40 days or a few more or a few less, and all this is according to the diversity of the complexities and of the other dispositions.
Chapter XV.

Chapter XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick, and it is called by the name of the tibia; and the other is thin, and it is called fibula.

And if the two bones are broken together, the leg is converted to all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is converted to the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it turns back, and it is of those causes that are not to your liking.
And then administer extension and equation and ligation of the splints according to that alignment.
But however, if the fracture of the leg is large having many stages, that the extension be less and lighter, and alleviate with your virtue.

And most importantly, that you do this in a fracture, and when with that it is properly cured.

Chapter .xix.

Chapter .xx.
Of the cure of the swelling that occurs in the region of any fracture.
Often this swelling occurs in the region of the healing of a fracture, and most often it is near the joints, and it is disfigured by it the figure of the limb; and very often it forbids the limb from its natural operation.
Therefore consider if the swelling is recent and administer in that those medicines that have stipulated as such as aloe, and incense, and mira, and wormwood, and similar to those; in such a way that you take some of those or all of them, and combine those with vi stiptic, or with album of egg, or with vi agre, and place that over the swelling in a cloth, and bind those over it with a good constriction.
And leave the constriction that does not untie that for many days; and then untie that and iterate another, until the swelling is reduced; or strengthen over that a plate of lead, because lead has the property of reducing everything that is inflated in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already been converted into a fester and it is necessary to provoke to drain that wound, then cut that open along the most sovereign part of it, and incise the superfluity and eminence, or drain that with some reeds until it is reduced; and cure that until it is healed.
Chapter .xxi.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and then the limb becomes subtle and flexible again according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then the limb becomes subtle and flexible again, then this is not done except for many causes: of which one is the multitude of solution of the ligament and of the ligament of it made not according to what it should be; or for superfluity of constriction of the ligaments while the nourishment is prohibited from running to the limb; or for multitude of superfluous embrocation; or for superfluous movement not at the right time; or for lack of blood in the body of the patient and for weakness of it.

And then place a poultice on the limb, so that the poultice attracts nourishment to it, or continue embrocation of it with warm water, until the nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter .xxii.

Chapter .xxiii.
Of the treatment of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from its place, so that it is prohibited from movement, and the figure of the limb is disfigured, and it brings upon the patient pains and great injuries.
Therefore, when it happens to someone a dislocation, then it is necessary to immediately perforate that it be reduced, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the place is apostematized and very much with that it is made difficult the reduction of dislocation.
And for this, it is not necessary that it be bad or stretched in the hour of the apostematization, because many times it brings spasms and terrible pains.
But when this happens, then it is necessary that you get to the flaubotomia of the patient, and then let it rest until the apostematize rests; and then the limb be embrocated with warm water and oil, and then be reduced with facility.
And every member be cured with this, of which the memory will come back in its place.
And you already have ordered the capitals of dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the part of the body most sovereign to the part most left.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of dislocation of the jaw left.
So dislocated are the two jaws if no tart; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: that or they are remaugulated from their places by little movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by entire dislocation and complicated, until they are softened at the junction, until saliva of the patient flows and that he cannot have that nor can he close his mouth, and the tongue of him be enveloped in his word.
But when the dislocation of him is little, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complicated, then it is necessary that it be administered reduction of it lightly, and not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister has the head of the patient, and the doctor inserts his finger of one hand into the root of the dislocation of the jaw from within his mouth, if the dislocation is of one part; or that he inserts his two fingers together, if the dislocation is of two parts, and the others of his hands are outside with which he grasps; and he commands the patient to cease his jaws and let those go from each part; and the doctor grasps the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if it is difficult the reduction of it, and most if both the jaws are dislocated at once, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil, until it is made lighter the reduction of both and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.

And this be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth of the patient, the eating and drinking and spitting, until the jaw be nozzled and healed.

Chapter XXVI.

Chapter .xxvii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the hip.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all parts, and more so in the front part than in the back part, and the dislocation of it is not known until it is touched or felt, according to which figure it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated hip to its place, this is declared to you manifestly, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch with its own muscle.
And cover that hip, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before an abscess comes to it, because if it comes to an abscess, it is difficult to reduce it, and it is not healed of all; and especially when it is in the back part, because it is the worst of all kinds of dislocations, and of greater pain, and many times they leave to it death.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand of it be extended with two fingers, and the arm of it be expanded, and the hands of the physician be over the hip and below it, and he pushes the joint with the tips of his fingers, or at the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front part, then it returns as if you bend the hand with your hand, until you strike the hip with the root of your palm.

And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and hold that elevation of the ministers so that it is not removed from the extension.

And after it is loosened; and therefore if the joint is formed in its place, then you untie the bandage of it and leave it.

And if you wish, place upon it a piece of damp cowhide scraped with border and strengthen upon it; and do this several times, because it is moist and reduces it to its disposition.
Chapter .xxviii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
Wrist dislocation sometimes occurs, and the reduction of its dislocation is lighter, and the opposite is true of other joints, but however, cover that it is velocity in the reduction of it in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place is suppurated or that which happens in it suppuration.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the joint of the hand of the patient upon a table and the minister extends the hand of that, and the physician places his hand or his palm upon the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its place.
But however, cover that it is considered if the palm is already placed on the back of the hand, then the patient places the back of his hand upon a table with the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is preceding of the back of the hand, then be the position of the hand of that intrinsically upon the table, so that the hand of the physician sits upon that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns quickly, it is good; and if not, then strengthen it with a bandage yielding the suppuration and leave it; and do not repeat this, because if not, it does not suffer nor can you upon the reduction of it more than passing to it days.
But the joint is straightened according to its crookedness; and no cause is given to the patient, except that the hand is softened and cannot tighten any cause; then you know that the nerves are cut or torn, and then there is no need for it except to tighten it with a cautery near the place of it and firmly apply water to it, and give no cause.
Therefore, when the bad flesh is returned, then place a poultice on it which we have prepared, and let it be left and tightened for 5 days; and then remove it and let it be left and the hand of it be exercised.
But if the movement of it is stayed and there is any heaviness in it, then gently moisten it with warm water and with friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the cure of dislocations of the joints.
Sometimes joints are dislocated in every part.
And when a finger is dislocated of those in the palm of the hand or on the back of it, then straighten the finger and impel the dislocation of it with the thumbs, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that part to which it is dislocated, and let it be for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until it is straight and equal in that day.
And when night comes, tie that according to that narration, and do not cease to untie that in the day and exercise with movement, and tie that at night; and do this for some days until it is straight.
And do similar with this, if it is dislocated on the back of the hand, and tie that to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated in the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
Of the cure of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When there occurs to any of the vertebrae of the back or of the neck a complicated dislocation, and the movable vertebrae are removed from their places, then in that there is no cure, because death seizes the patient.
And a sign of this is that the digestion of the patient is outside of its will, and it cannot retain that; and many times are softened some limbs of it or two legs of it or two arms of it, or one of those; or is removed a vertebra from its place, because many times is removed and many times is made the removal of those in four parts.
Therefore, that is already removed to the rear parts and is called gelbozit.
But the cure of it is that then you consider if the gelbozit occurs from puerility, because then in that there is no cure nor is there healing of all.
But in that which comes from accident or from percussion or similar to those, the Ancients already said the ways of cure with verbose sermons, of which the most returns to prophecy; but I have already abridged of that of which the little is excused by the excess of it, for my better proximity to the better understanding exposition, with which I have this explained and formed from diverse instruments of that with which I will show this and expose that.
And of those, there is no cure except that which occurs in the back properly with that which I have recounted.
And it is that the patient be extended on his face, with his legs straight and close to the wall, and be extended below him, with a soft covering so that the feet of him are not lifted.
And then a straight stick be placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the leg near the head of it, and another stick in the opposite direction of the feet of it at the other end of the arm.
And the minister holds the stick, and it is not fixed in a ditch; and another minister holds the other one according to that form.
And then envelop over the head of the patient and below the titillated limbs, lean firmly; and extend the extremity of the limb to the stick which is opposite the head of it, and tie that in it; and then tighten the other limb over the hips of it the denols of it, and opposite the legs of it; and then adjust all the limbs and tie those in the other stick which is opposite the feet of it.
And then the minister, having covered the limb to the part of the stick, and not being removed the two sticks and the limbs from their places in which they are fixed, but however, are not fixed as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms over the vertebra with force until it returns, or that which is placed on it a table, and then that he adjusts on the table his feet until it returns.
But if it does not return by this form, then he takes a table of which the width or length is opposite three arms, and hollows in the part of which we have said that it is near the patient a place in which enters the extremity of the table. And then places the majority of the table over the vertebra, the doctor places his feet on the other extremity, and tightens well until the vertebra is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open a turn which is revolved with the hand; and that you fix on the ground the head of the patient in the rear of the fist two rods, the length of which be of an arm, and fix the head of him in the rear of the fist also two rods; and be the length that is between each rod a palm, and already is made in each rod a turn in which runs the turn, and each one be fixed on the ground so that it be not bad.
And be it a rod round in which is the turn, and be revolved in the two rods of the two rods.
And in the extremity of it is a rod in which is formed the rod, the length of which is a palm with what is revolved; and in the two other rods is similar to that.
And then strengthen the ligaments that you have straightened in the foot of the patient in the rod which is of the head of him, and the ligaments that you have straightened in the thighs of him in the turn which is of the feet of him.
And then of each turn and stay a minister that twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor aligns the spine according to what we have said.
And as soon as the spine is returned, and the place is aligned, then cover with a paste made of dried albumen of egg, and then with cotton; and then place over the paste a board of wood whose width be three fingers or about those, and the length of it be as much as the width of the place of the swelling, and on the part of the spine its.
And then tie it with the ligament which we have said, and use the patient of subtle nourishment until he be healed.
And if there remains any swelling in the place at the end of the healing, then cover that with medicines that soften and cleanse with administration of the board for a long time, which we have recommended; and already is administered in that place a plate of lead.
And some times it happens that swelling occurs in the rear of the two spondils, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells because it should not be done with this medicine, for it will very much cause you to die.
Chapter .XXXI.
Of the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle should not be dislocated only, and it does not happen to those what happens to the other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated to the front parts, or to the back parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And most often it is dislocated to the front parts, and few to the front parts or to the back parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it to the front parts is that, when you adjust the leg of the healthy patient with the sick one, it is longer and the thigh is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his leg towards the knee, and it is the place that if the knee is bent, an abscess forms, because the head of the thigh has already arrived here.
And the sign of the one to which the dislocation happens to the back parts is that the accidents of it are contrary to this accident.
And the sign of the one to which the dislocation happens to the front parts is that it expands its thigh according to its completion, however it does not bend that which causes pain in the thigh, and if it tries to go and cannot go over that to the front parts; and the urine of him is retained and the groin of him is formed with an abscess, and the ambulation of him is on the heel.
And the sign of the one to which the dislocation happens to the back parts is that it does not expand the thigh, nor can it bend that which bends the groin, and the thigh of him is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is of that place.
But the reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and antique, that is already fixed with that having it, then you administer reduction of it and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of all; and therefore it should not be done with this.
But in the one whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four species of dislocation, then you take care to what you return the joint and extend it to the front parts and to the back parts, and move it to the right and left, for very much it returns and does not need other cure.
But, if it does not return in this way, then you must prepare a strong minister and extend the thigh of him from the farther part, or with his hands, or with a ligament which is tied above the thigh; and another minister extends the hand of him from below so that it puts his hands below the buttocks of him; and then tighten with a ligament on the root of the thigh, and hold another minister the extremity of the ligament.
And be extension of him or from the front part to the groin to the furcula, or from the back part to the part of the buttocks.
And the extension of all those in a single time while the patient is raised with their body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is special and common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to all kinds of what I will say of the proper cure.
In the reduction of it own, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is at the parts between, so that the patient is laid back on their side; and then place the ligaments over the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is left the groin; and then extend the ligaments upwards from the part of the groin to the highest part of the clavicle.
And then another minister strongly presses the arms of him, and  amerges the thick part of the thigh of the patient and extends to the forward parts by vehement extension, so that that returns to its place.
And this kind of cure is of the lightest with which this limb is reduced than the others.
But if it is excused over you and do not obey the entrance with this kind of cure of all, then it is necessary that the two feet of the patient be laid together with a strong, light ligament, on both the ankles and both the knees.
And the length of each of its parts is the quantity of four fingers, and the leg of the patient extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient is suspended over the head of the pole, and that it is in the middle; and the length of him from the ground is the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to pull the head of the thigh, and that it is suspended with the patient another young man, and the other young man pulls the thigh with strength and impulsion, so that the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is at the outward part, so that the patient lies on his back as we have recounted in the having a gelbozit; and be strengthened the ligament over the leg of the patient properly and over the feet of him.
And then two poles are placed, one at the feet of him, and the other at the head; and then poles are placed adjusted in the middle of the chest laid upon which damp cloths are enveloped, so that the patient is not harmed because the pole is between the thighs of him, because you must the extension to the forward parts not be pulled.
And then each minister extends to his part, and the doctor with his hand engulfs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is at the forward parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration about chest; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand over the groin of the patient, and then shortens that with the other hand, and with that shortening places that extended to the forward parts to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is at the backward parts, then it is necessary that the patient be not extended to the forward parts, and it is elevated above the ground; having it necessary that it be placed upon a hard cause, as well as if the displacement is at the outward parts, according to what we have said of the placing of him upon two planks; and it is upon his back and the ligaments are extended, according to what we have said, there is no harm.
And so that compression be administered with a table over the place where the joint is.
Therefore, as the reduction is complicated according to what is necessary—the sign of the reduction of him is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend from the patient two legs, and when you see those engulfs and the patient extends his leg and expands that ses excusacio, then you know that the limb according to what is necessary is returned—and then you adjust two thighs and place plaster and extend with a bandage so that the width is not harmed in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or for four.
And then untie the ligament and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see those engulfs in good condition, then you know that the dislocation is already healed, and therefore leave the patient to go.
And if you see any cause of mollification, then you return and plaster that, and extend that according to your first structure, and leave that in three days.
And then untie that, and make him delay in ambulation over some for some days, until he is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated according to three ways, for it is dislocated at the outward parts, and at the inward parts, and at the forward parts more to the backward parts; and at the forward parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of him is that you command the patient to apply his leg to the thigh; then you know that the bone is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the manners of him is that you make the patient straight, so that already extends his leg, if possible; adhere your two hands to the face of him, and pause his foot between your feet; and then make adhere the bone of him to your palms, and retain those between your fingers on the bone of him with strength, and another minister extends his leg while the bone returns to its place.
And the sign of return of him is that his leg adheres to the thigh with light no-laborous.
And then bandage that and make adhere the leg to the thigh, and then tie those together immediately with a bandage for three days or four, and then untie those.
And do not use the patient of walking for some days, until he is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of him to you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments of which in the cure of the hernia preceding narration.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the talus.
The talus some times is dislocated by small motion, and some times is dislocated according to compliment, and the dislocation of him is made either to the parts behind or to the parts before.
And the sign of the dislocation of him is that sometimes the talus inflated proceeds to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of the dislocation of him is of light reduction, and it is that it be extended with ease with the hands and be enveloped while it returns.
But in the cure of him when it is dislocated according to compliment, cove that you make the patient straight, and have that one strong and valiant minister of the part behind of that in the middle place of him.
And then you with your right hand the foot of the most sovereign part, and with your left hand of the part most behind of the foot in the place of the cavity.
And then pull the foot to you with your right hand, and with the left two times, and then extend with the left; and push the foot of the foot with the right, so that the leg works.
And do this twice according to that we have counted.
And then push the foot of the foot to the leg the third time, and you are in the cavity.
Therefore if it returns in one time or two according to this manner, and if sometimes the foot enveloped, it is good; and if not, then over him iterates the operation, that he returns.
But if to you is forbidden the reduction of him with that we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a piece of cloth on the ground firm, and that it lies between the thighs of him, and you already have enveloped over him cloth so that it does not scratch the patient.
And then the minister of him hold his thighs, and another minister extends the leg with his hands or with a ligament that lies on the neck of the foot; and then extend that each minister against extension of his part, and the cloth be extant between the thighs of the patient retaining that, so that the body of him not be pulled to the parts behind of his extension.
And then the physician envelops the dislocation with his hands, and another minister holds the sound thigh to the parts behind, so that the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore when it returns the dislocation and is declared to you the health of the reduction of him, then pause plaster and stretch that with strong ligaments, and tie the foot with ligaments to the leg.
And cove that you fear or you guard yourself of the nerves which are behind on the talus, so that the ligament over that not be vehement and hurt that strongly.
And then leave that for two days or three; and if the ligament is softened, stretch that.
And then untie that on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient from movement for 40 days. But if it is forced to go before that space, it is not done safe that the dislocation not be over him dissolved and corrupted, and then that which does not receive cure.
And if it happens to him abscess caut, then cove that you administer in the reduction of him that in the place where we preceded our narration, in another place of cure and embrocation, until it is remauged.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the digits of the feet.
It cove that the one which is dislocated be enveloped with little extension ses trebal; and that which is not difficult, abantz is leugier.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the leg of the foot, then cove that you make the patient his foot on a flat place of the ground or on a table, and be standing thus as if he was going.
And then you stand on weight, and pause your foot over that which imineys of the joints, and then step that with your foot with virtue until it returns, and that you see that already enveloped and does not appear in the place eminence.
And then place under the patient's foot a board that supports the entire foot, which has two ends, and then strengthen it with a structure decent and firm for three days; and then untie it and make the patient refrain from walking for several days, until it is healed and the recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
Of the kinds of dislocations that are made with plague or with fracture or with any of them together.
When any of those causes occur, and you immerse yourself in the cure of it and in its restoration, many times death occurs.
And for this reason, no one should presume over the cure of it, but rather he who is wise in the art, of long usage, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not rash nor presumptuous; and who uses in the beginning of medicines that cause abscesses only so much, and leaves the patient to the divine judgment, but rather to him whom you hope for salvation from perdition, with the alleviation of the disease, and in which appears some hope. And therefore, strive as soon as possible to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a cauterized abscess.
And therefore, if the limb returns as we want, then administer the regimen which causes a cauterized abscess and cures the plague with what is convenient to it of the dried herbs.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the broken bones are separated, then you penetrate the stem of them, and do what we have said in the simple ailments of this which is preceded in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the instrument in the ways of fear according to what has been preceded to you my preaching; for this is better to you, you should owe your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more greetings to your true body those in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasim perfectly of which may God be the benefit. Amen.
